## Chapter: Chapter 5: Lower Mantle Petrology

### Introduction

The Earth's mantle, a region between the crust and the core, is a complex and dynamic system that has undergone significant thermal and chemical evolution over billions of years. In this chapter, we delve into the lower mantle, a region that extends from a depth of approximately 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2900 km. The lower mantle is a critical component of the Earth's interior, playing a pivotal role in the planet's thermal and chemical evolution.

The lower mantle is characterized by extreme pressure and temperature conditions, which have a profound influence on the physical and chemical properties of the mantle. These conditions have led to the formation of unique mineral assemblages and the development of complex geochemical processes. Understanding these processes is crucial for comprehending the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution.

In this chapter, we will explore the petrology of the lower mantle, focusing on the mineral composition, physical properties, and geochemical processes that occur within this region. We will also discuss the role of the lower mantle in the Earth's overall thermal and chemical evolution, including its interaction with the upper mantle and the core.

Through this exploration, we aim to provide a comprehensive understanding of the lower mantle, shedding light on the complex processes that have shaped the Earth's interior over time. This knowledge is not only of academic interest but also has practical implications, as it can inform our understanding of other terrestrial planets and the processes that occur within them.

As we journey through the lower mantle, we will encounter a world of extreme conditions and fascinating geological phenomena. This chapter aims to make this journey accessible and engaging, providing a comprehensive and accessible introduction to the petrology of the lower mantle.




### Subsection: 4.3b Effects of Metasomatism on Mantle Composition

Metasomatism has profound effects on the composition of the Earth's mantle. The process of metasomatism can alter the mineralogy, chemical composition, and thermal properties of the mantle, leading to significant changes in the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution.

#### 4.3b.1 Alteration of Mineralogy

Metasomatism can significantly alter the mineralogy of the mantle. The introduction of hydrothermal fluids can lead to the dissolution and precipitation of new minerals, resulting in the formation of new rock types. For example, in the contact metamorphic aureole, the hydrothermal fluids can dissolve minerals in the intrusive rock and deposit them in the surrounding metamorphic rocks, leading to the formation of skarns. This process can also occur in the mantle, leading to the formation of new mineral assemblages.

#### 4.3b.2 Changes in Chemical Composition

Metasomatism can also lead to significant changes in the chemical composition of the mantle. The hydrothermal fluids can transport elements and compounds from the source rock to the surrounding rocks, leading to the redistribution of these elements. This can result in the enrichment or depletion of certain elements in the mantle, depending on the composition of the hydrothermal fluids and the source rock.

#### 4.3b.3 Impact on Thermal Properties

The thermal properties of the mantle can also be affected by metasomatism. The introduction of hydrothermal fluids can alter the thermal conductivity of the mantle, leading to changes in the heat flow from the Earth's interior. This can have implications for the Earth's thermal evolution, as changes in the heat flow can affect the rates of mantle convection and the movement of heat through the mantle.

#### 4.3b.4 Role in the Earth's Thermal and Chemical Evolution

The effects of metasomatism on the mantle composition are not isolated events, but rather part of a larger process of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. The changes in mineralogy, chemical composition, and thermal properties brought about by metasomatism can influence other processes, such as mantle convection and the formation of new crust. Understanding the effects of metasomatism on the mantle is therefore crucial for a comprehensive exploration of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution.




### Subsection: 4.3c Case Studies of Metasomatism

In this section, we will explore some case studies of metasomatism to further understand its effects on mantle composition. These case studies will provide real-world examples and insights into the processes involved in metasomatism.

#### 4.3c.1 Gore Mountain Garnet

The Gore Mountain Garnet is a well-known example of metasomatism in the Earth's mantle. The protolith, a spinel-clouded plagioclase metamorphosed gabbro (metagabbro), underwent metamorphism during the Ottawan phase of the Grenville orogeny. The peak temperature of metamorphism was notably high, being over 900 °C. Peak pressure was also quite high, ranging from 9-10 kbar.

The metamorphosis of the protolith into garnet amphibolite is marked by orogenic folding and plastic flow, as well as a hydration phase. Garnet growth, introduction of fluid, retrograde metamorphism, and ductile deformation all occurred simultaneously. The fluid flow was likely channelized, as evidence from the large variability in trace elements. Fluid flow induced ion mobility and plasticity, as well as enriching the whole rock chemistry with lithium and thorium and depleting cesium.

The Gore Mountain Garnet case study highlights the profound effects of metasomatism on mantle composition. The introduction of hydrothermal fluids led to significant changes in the mineralogy, chemical composition, and thermal properties of the mantle. This case study also demonstrates the role of metasomatism in the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution.

#### 4.3c.2 Messier 22

Another interesting case study of metasomatism is the Messier 22 cluster. This cluster, located in the constellation Sagittarius, is a globular cluster known for its high concentration of metal-rich stars. The cluster is believed to have formed from a single cloud of gas and dust, and its high metal content is thought to be the result of metasomatism.

The metasomatism in Messier 22 is believed to have occurred early in the cluster's history, during the process of stellar birth. The high metal content of the cluster is thought to be the result of the metasomatism, which involved the introduction of hydrothermal fluids and the subsequent redistribution of elements and compounds.

The Messier 22 case study provides further evidence of the profound effects of metasomatism on mantle composition. The introduction of hydrothermal fluids can lead to significant changes in the chemical composition of the mantle, as seen in the high metal content of Messier 22. This case study also highlights the role of metasomatism in the formation of globular clusters, which are some of the oldest objects in the universe.

#### 4.3c.3 3C 273

The quasar 3C 273 is another example of metasomatism in the Earth's mantle. This quasar, located in the constellation Virgo, is known for its high redshift and is believed to be one of the most distant objects in the universe. The metasomatism in 3C 273 is believed to have occurred early in the quasar's history, during the process of galaxy formation.

The metasomatism in 3C 273 is believed to have involved the introduction of hydrothermal fluids and the subsequent redistribution of elements and compounds. This process led to significant changes in the chemical composition of the mantle, resulting in the high metal content observed in 3C 273.

The 3C 273 case study provides further evidence of the profound effects of metasomatism on mantle composition. The introduction of hydrothermal fluids can lead to significant changes in the chemical composition of the mantle, as seen in 3C 273. This case study also highlights the role of metasomatism in the formation of quasars, which are some of the most energetic objects in the universe.

### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored some case studies of metasomatism to further understand its effects on mantle composition. These case studies have provided real-world examples and insights into the processes involved in metasomatism. The Gore Mountain Garnet, Messier 22, and 3C 273 case studies have demonstrated the profound effects of metasomatism on mantle composition, including changes in mineralogy, chemical composition, and thermal properties. These case studies also highlight the role of metasomatism in the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution.

We have learned about the composition of the mantle, its structure, and the various factors that influence its evolution. From the chemical reactions that occur deep within the mantle to the role of plate tectonics in mantle dynamics, we have gained a comprehensive understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle.

We have also examined the various types of mantle, including the upper mantle and lower mantle, and the differences between them. We have learned about the role of mantle plumes and their impact on mantle convection, as well as the importance of mantle xenoliths in our understanding of mantle petrology.

In conclusion, the study of mantle petrology is a vast and complex field, but one that is crucial to our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. By studying the mantle, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years, and continue to shape it today.

## Chapter: Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of the Earth's lower mantle. The lower mantle is a region of the Earth's interior that extends from a depth of approximately 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2900 km. It is a dynamic and complex environment, where heat and chemical reactions shape the evolution of our planet. 

The lower mantle is a critical component of the Earth's thermal and chemical system. It is the region where the majority of the Earth's heat is generated and transferred, playing a crucial role in the planet's overall energy balance. The lower mantle is also a site of intense chemical reactions, where minerals and elements are transformed under extreme conditions of temperature and pressure. 

In this chapter, we will explore the physical and chemical properties of the lower mantle, including its composition, structure, and dynamics. We will also examine the processes that drive the evolution of the lower mantle, such as convection, subduction, and chemical reactions. 

Our exploration of the lower mantle will be guided by the latest research and advancements in geophysics, geochemistry, and geodynamics. We will also draw on a wealth of data from seismic waves, mineral inclusions, and laboratory experiments to paint a comprehensive picture of the lower mantle. 

Join us as we journey into the depths of the Earth, to unravel the mysteries of the lower mantle and its role in the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet.


# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

## Chapter 5: Lower Mantle

 5.1: Lower Mantle Composition

The lower mantle is a region of the Earth's interior that extends from a depth of approximately 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2900 km. It is a dynamic and complex environment, where heat and chemical reactions shape the evolution of our planet. In this section, we will explore the composition of the lower mantle, including its major and minor components, and the processes that drive its evolution.

#### 5.1a: Major and Minor Components of Lower Mantle

The lower mantle is primarily composed of silicate minerals, with the most abundant being olivine, pyroxene, and feldspar. These minerals are formed under extreme conditions of temperature and pressure, and their presence in the lower mantle provides valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

Olivine is a common mineral in the lower mantle, accounting for approximately 40% of the mantle's composition. It is a magnesium-iron silicate with a simple crystal structure, making it stable at high pressures and temperatures. Olivine is also a major component of the Earth's upper mantle, and its presence in the lower mantle suggests that there is significant mixing between the upper and lower mantle.

Pyroxene is another common mineral in the lower mantle, accounting for approximately 30% of the mantle's composition. It is a calcium-magnesium silicate with a more complex crystal structure than olivine. Pyroxene is stable at higher pressures and temperatures than olivine, making it more prevalent in the lower mantle. Its presence in the lower mantle is a key indicator of the high temperatures and pressures found in this region.

Feldspar is a minor component of the lower mantle, accounting for approximately 10% of the mantle's composition. It is a potassium-sodium silicate with a complex crystal structure. Feldspar is stable at lower pressures and temperatures than olivine and pyroxene, making its presence in the lower mantle a strong indication of the mixing of upper and lower mantle materials.

In addition to these major components, the lower mantle also contains minor components such as iron, aluminum, and titanium. These elements are present in trace amounts, but their presence can provide valuable insights into the chemical reactions occurring in the lower mantle.

The composition of the lower mantle is not static, but rather evolves over time due to various processes. These processes include convection, subduction, and chemical reactions. Convection is the movement of mantle materials due to differences in temperature and density, while subduction is the process by which oceanic crust is recycled into the mantle. Chemical reactions also play a crucial role in the evolution of the lower mantle, as they can alter the composition of mantle materials and drive the formation of new minerals.

In conclusion, the composition of the lower mantle is a complex and dynamic system, shaped by a variety of processes. The major and minor components of the lower mantle provide valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth, and their study continues to be a topic of great interest in the field of geophysics. 


# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

## Chapter 5: Lower Mantle

 5.1: Lower Mantle Composition

The lower mantle is a region of the Earth's interior that extends from a depth of approximately 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2900 km. It is a dynamic and complex environment, where heat and chemical reactions shape the evolution of our planet. In this section, we will explore the composition of the lower mantle, including its major and minor components, and the processes that drive its evolution.

These minerals are formed under extreme conditions of temperature and pressure, and their presence in the lower mantle provides valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

Olivine is a common mineral in the lower mantle, accounting for approximately 40% of the mantle's composition. It is a magnesium-iron silicate with a simple crystal structure, making it stable at high pressures and temperatures. Olivine is also a major component of the Earth's upper mantle, and its presence in the lower mantle suggests that there is significant mixing between the upper and lower mantle.

Pyroxene is another common mineral in the lower mantle, accounting for approximately 30% of the mantle's composition. It is a calcium-magnesium silicate with a more complex crystal structure than olivine. Pyroxene is stable at higher pressures and temperatures than olivine, making it more prevalent in the lower mantle. Its presence in the lower mantle is a key indicator of the high temperatures and pressures found in this region.

Feldspar is a minor component of the lower mantle, accounting for approximately 10% of the mantle's composition. It is a potassium-sodium silicate with a complex crystal structure. Feldspar is stable at lower pressures and temperatures than olivine and pyroxene, making its presence in the lower mantle a strong indication of the mixing of upper and lower mantle materials.

In addition to these major components, the lower mantle also contains minor components such as iron, aluminum, and titanium. These elements are present in trace amounts, but their presence can provide valuable insights into the chemical reactions occurring in the lower mantle.

#### 5.1b: Lower Mantle Composition and Evolution

The composition of the lower mantle is not static, but rather evolves over time due to various processes. These processes include convection, subduction, and chemical reactions. Convection is the movement of mantle materials due to differences in temperature and density, while subduction is the process by which oceanic crust is recycled into the mantle. Chemical reactions also play a crucial role in the evolution of the lower mantle, as they can alter the composition of mantle materials and drive the formation of new minerals.

One of the key processes driving the evolution of the lower mantle is the process of subduction. As oceanic crust is recycled into the mantle, it brings with it a unique composition of elements and minerals. This can lead to changes in the overall composition of the lower mantle, as well as the formation of new minerals.

Chemical reactions also play a significant role in the evolution of the lower mantle. As mantle materials are subjected to extreme conditions of temperature and pressure, they can undergo chemical reactions that alter their composition. These reactions can also lead to the formation of new minerals, further adding to the complexity of the lower mantle's composition.

In conclusion, the composition of the lower mantle is a dynamic and complex system, shaped by various processes such as convection, subduction, and chemical reactions. By studying the major and minor components of the lower mantle, as well as the processes driving its evolution, we can gain a deeper understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

## Chapter 5: Lower Mantle




### Subsection: 4.4a Introduction to Geochemical Tracers

Geochemical tracers are substances that can be used to trace the movement of fluids and the processes that they undergo. These tracers are particularly useful in the study of mantle petrology, as they can provide insights into the composition and evolution of the Earth's mantle. In this section, we will explore the role of geochemical tracers in understanding mantle processes.

#### 4.4a.1 Isotopic Tracers

Isotopic tracers are substances whose isotopic composition can be used to trace the movement of fluids. Isotopes of the same element have different atomic masses due to variations in the number of neutrons in their nucleus. These variations can be used to track the movement of fluids, as different isotopes can have different rates of reaction and diffusion.

For example, the isotopic composition of oxygen can be used to trace the movement of fluids in the mantle. The isotope <sup>18</sup>O is heavier than <sup>16</sup>O and <sup>17</sup>O, and it is preferentially removed from a fluid during evaporation. This means that fluids that have undergone evaporation will have a higher proportion of <sup>16</sup>O and <sup>17</sup>O, while fluids that have not undergone evaporation will have a higher proportion of <sup>18</sup>O. By measuring the isotopic composition of oxygen in mantle rocks, scientists can determine the extent of evaporation that has occurred.

#### 4.4a.2 Trace Elements

Trace elements are elements that occur in very small quantities in a given system. They are often present in concentrations of less than 0.1% by weight, but they can have a significant impact on the overall composition of a system. Trace elements can be used as tracers because they can be introduced into a system through various processes, such as volcanic eruptions or hydrothermal activity.

For example, the element lithium is often used as a trace element in mantle studies. Lithium is a highly reactive element, and it is often introduced into the mantle through hydrothermal activity. By measuring the concentration of lithium in mantle rocks, scientists can determine the extent of hydrothermal activity that has occurred.

#### 4.4a.3 Applications of Geochemical Tracers

Geochemical tracers have a wide range of applications in mantle petrology. They can be used to trace the movement of fluids, determine the extent of various processes, and provide insights into the composition and evolution of the mantle.

For example, the isotopic composition of oxygen can be used to trace the movement of fluids in the mantle. By measuring the isotopic composition of oxygen in mantle rocks, scientists can determine the extent of evaporation that has occurred. This can provide insights into the processes that have shaped the mantle over time.

Trace elements, such as lithium, can also be used to trace the movement of fluids in the mantle. By measuring the concentration of lithium in mantle rocks, scientists can determine the extent of hydrothermal activity that has occurred. This can provide insights into the role of hydrothermal activity in the evolution of the mantle.

In conclusion, geochemical tracers are powerful tools in the study of mantle petrology. They can provide insights into the composition and evolution of the mantle, and they can help us understand the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. In the following sections, we will explore some specific examples of how geochemical tracers have been used in mantle studies.





### Subsection: 4.4b Application of Geochemical Tracers in Mantle Processes

Geochemical tracers have been instrumental in advancing our understanding of mantle processes. By providing insights into the composition and evolution of the mantle, these tracers have helped to shed light on the complex dynamics of the Earth's interior.

#### 4.4b.1 Isotopic Tracers in Mantle Processes

Isotopic tracers have been particularly useful in studying mantle processes. For instance, the isotopic composition of oxygen has been used to trace the movement of fluids in the mantle. By studying the isotopic composition of oxygen in mantle rocks, scientists have been able to determine the extent of evaporation that has occurred, providing insights into the conditions under which these rocks were formed.

Similarly, the isotopic composition of hydrogen has been used to trace the movement of hydrogen in the mantle. Hydrogen is a key component of water, and its isotopic composition can provide insights into the presence and movement of water in the mantle. This has been particularly useful in understanding the role of water in mantle processes, such as the formation of diamonds.

#### 4.4b.2 Trace Elements in Mantle Processes

Trace elements have also been instrumental in studying mantle processes. For instance, the element lithium, which is often used as a trace element in mantle studies, has been found to be enriched in the mantle plumes that rise from the core-mantle boundary. This enrichment has been attributed to the presence of water in the mantle plumes, which can facilitate the release of lithium from minerals.

Other trace elements, such as strontium and neodymium, have been used to trace the movement of fluids in the mantle. These elements are often present in different proportions in different types of mantle rocks, and their distribution can provide insights into the processes that have shaped the mantle.

In conclusion, geochemical tracers have been instrumental in advancing our understanding of mantle processes. By providing insights into the composition and evolution of the mantle, these tracers have helped to shed light on the complex dynamics of the Earth's interior.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of mantle petrology, exploring the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. We have examined the composition of the mantle, its structure, and the processes that have shaped it over time. We have also explored the role of the mantle in the overall geological processes of the Earth, including plate tectonics and the formation of the continents.

The mantle, as we have seen, is a dynamic and complex system, constantly evolving and interacting with the other layers of the Earth. Its composition is not uniform, but varies with depth and location, reflecting the diverse processes that have acted upon it. The mantle's thermal evolution has been driven by the decay of radioactive elements and the residual heat from the Earth's formation, leading to changes in temperature and pressure that have influenced the mantle's chemical composition.

The study of mantle petrology is a vast and ongoing field, with many questions still to be answered. However, the knowledge and understanding we have gained so far have provided a solid foundation for further exploration and research. As we continue to unravel the mysteries of the mantle, we are gaining a deeper understanding of the Earth's past, present, and future.

## Chapter 5: Lower Mantle

### Introduction

The lower mantle, a region of the Earth's interior extending from a depth of approximately 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2900 km, is a critical component of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. This chapter will delve into the intricate details of the lower mantle, exploring its composition, structure, and the processes that have shaped it over time.

The lower mantle is a dynamic and complex system, characterized by high temperatures and pressures. It is primarily composed of silicate minerals, with the most abundant being the magnesium-iron silicate perovskite. However, recent studies suggest that the lower mantle may also contain significant amounts of iron and other trace elements, which can have profound implications for our understanding of the Earth's evolution.

The lower mantle plays a crucial role in the Earth's heat budget, acting as a thermal barrier between the hot core and the cooler upper mantle. The heat generated by the core is transferred through the lower mantle, influencing the geodynamo that generates the Earth's magnetic field. Understanding the thermal properties of the lower mantle is therefore essential for comprehending the Earth's magnetic history and its future.

In addition to its thermal role, the lower mantle also plays a key part in the chemical evolution of the Earth. It is here that the subducted oceanic crust and sediments are recycled, contributing to the overall composition of the mantle. The lower mantle also acts as a reservoir for volatile elements, such as water and carbon, which can have significant implications for the Earth's surface environment.

This chapter will explore these topics and more, providing a comprehensive exploration of the lower mantle and its role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Through a combination of theoretical analysis, observational data, and computational modeling, we will strive to gain a deeper understanding of this enigmatic region of the Earth's interior.




### Subsection: 4.4c Recent Advances in Geochemical Tracers Research

Recent advances in geochemical tracers research have further enhanced our understanding of mantle processes. These advancements have been driven by the development of new analytical techniques and the application of these techniques to the study of mantle rocks.

#### 4.4c.1 New Analytical Techniques

New analytical techniques have been developed that allow for the precise measurement of trace elements and isotopes in mantle rocks. These techniques include inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS), which allows for the measurement of trace elements with high sensitivity and precision, and cavity ring-down spectroscopy (CRDS), which allows for the measurement of isotopes with high precision and accuracy.

#### 4.4c.2 Application of New Analytical Techniques

The application of these new analytical techniques to the study of mantle rocks has led to significant advancements in our understanding of mantle processes. For instance, the use of ICP-MS has allowed for the detection of trace elements in mantle rocks at levels that were previously undetectable. This has provided new insights into the composition and evolution of the mantle.

Similarly, the use of CRDS has allowed for the precise measurement of isotopes in mantle rocks. This has led to the discovery of new isotopic variations in the mantle, which have provided insights into the movement of fluids in the mantle and the role of these fluids in mantle processes.

#### 4.4c.3 Recent Findings

Recent research using these new analytical techniques has led to several important findings. For instance, it has been found that the mantle is enriched in certain trace elements, such as lithium, which suggests the presence of water in the mantle. This finding has been corroborated by the discovery of hydrogen in mantle rocks, which further supports the presence of water in the mantle.

Furthermore, it has been found that the mantle is enriched in certain isotopes, such as oxygen-18 and hydrogen-2, which suggests the presence of fluids in the mantle. This finding has been corroborated by the discovery of fluid inclusions in mantle rocks, which have been found to contain high concentrations of these isotopes.

In conclusion, recent advances in geochemical tracers research have greatly enhanced our understanding of mantle processes. These advancements have been driven by the development of new analytical techniques and the application of these techniques to the study of mantle rocks. The future of geochemical tracers research in mantle studies looks promising, with the potential for even more significant advancements in our understanding of mantle processes.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of mantle petrology, exploring the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. We have examined the composition of the mantle, its structure, and the processes that have shaped it over time. We have also looked at the role of the mantle in the overall geological processes of the Earth, including plate tectonics and the formation of the continents.

The mantle, with its complex chemical and thermal properties, plays a crucial role in the Earth's evolution. Its composition is not uniform, but varies with depth and location. The upper mantle, for instance, is more silicate-rich than the lower mantle, which is more iron-rich. This variation is a result of the Earth's thermal evolution, with heat causing chemical reactions that alter the composition of the mantle.

The mantle also plays a key role in plate tectonics. The movement of tectonic plates is driven by the convection currents in the mantle, which are caused by the Earth's internal heat. This movement is responsible for the formation of new crust and the destruction of old crust, a process that is essential for the Earth's geological activity.

In conclusion, the study of mantle petrology is crucial for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It provides insights into the processes that have shaped the Earth over billions of years, and continues to shape it today.

The Earth's crust and mantle form the outermost layers of our planet, and they have been shaped by a complex interplay of thermal and chemical processes over billions of years. In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of the lower crust and mantle, exploring their composition, structure, and the dynamic processes that have shaped them.

The lower crust and mantle are not distinct layers, but rather a continuum of rocky material that extends from the base of the crust to the core-mantle boundary. This region is characterized by extreme pressure and temperature conditions, which have led to the formation of unique mineral assemblages and chemical reactions. Understanding these processes is crucial for comprehending the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

We will begin by examining the composition of the lower crust and mantle, exploring the minerals and elements that make up these layers. We will then delve into the structure of these layers, discussing the different types of rocks and their distribution. We will also explore the processes that drive the movement of material within the lower crust and mantle, including convection and subduction.

# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:




### Introduction

The study of noble gases has been a crucial aspect of understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Noble gases, also known as inert gases, are a group of chemical elements in the periodic table that have very low chemical reactivity due to their stable electron configurations. These gases are often found in the Earth's atmosphere, and their study has provided valuable insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

In this chapter, we will delve into the various aspects of noble gas constraints and their significance in the study of the Earth's evolution. We will explore the different types of noble gases, their sources, and their role in understanding the Earth's history. We will also discuss the techniques used to measure noble gases and the challenges faced in their analysis.

The study of noble gases has been a rapidly evolving field, with new techniques and technologies constantly being developed. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the current state of knowledge in this field, while also highlighting the ongoing research and future directions in the study of noble gases.

As we explore the noble gas constraints, we will also touch upon the broader implications of these findings for our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. This chapter will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the specific aspects of noble gas constraints and their applications in the study of the Earth's evolution.




### Subsection: 5.1b Noble Gas Distribution in the Mantle

The mantle is a crucial component of the Earth's interior, extending from a depth of approximately 800 km to the surface. It is composed primarily of silicate minerals, with a small percentage of noble gases. The distribution of noble gases in the mantle is of great interest to geologists and geophysicists, as it provides valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

#### 5.1b.1 Sources of Noble Gases in the Mantle

The primary source of noble gases in the mantle is the Earth's core. The core is believed to contain a significant amount of noble gases, primarily helium and neon, which are thought to have been released from the core during the early stages of the Earth's formation. This release is believed to have occurred due to the high temperatures and pressures in the core, which caused the noble gases to diffuse out.

Another source of noble gases in the mantle is the Earth's crust. The crust is constantly being recycled into the mantle through processes such as subduction and mantle convection. As the crust is recycled, it brings with it noble gases from the atmosphere and hydrosphere, which are then incorporated into the mantle.

#### 5.1b.2 Distribution of Noble Gases in the Mantle

The distribution of noble gases in the mantle is not uniform. Helium and neon, which are believed to originate from the core, are found in higher concentrations in the lower mantle. This is due to the fact that these gases are more soluble in the iron-rich core than in the silicate minerals of the mantle. As a result, they are more likely to be found in the core-mantle boundary region.

On the other hand, noble gases such as argon and krypton, which are believed to originate from the crust, are found in higher concentrations in the upper mantle. This is because these gases are more soluble in the silicate minerals of the mantle than in the iron-rich core. As a result, they are more likely to be found in the upper mantle, where they are being recycled from the crust.

#### 5.1b.3 Implications of Noble Gas Distribution in the Mantle

The distribution of noble gases in the mantle has significant implications for our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. The presence of noble gases in the mantle provides evidence for the early release of gases from the core, which is crucial for understanding the formation of the Earth's atmosphere and oceans.

Furthermore, the distribution of noble gases in the mantle can also provide insights into the processes of mantle convection and subduction. By studying the noble gas distribution, geologists and geophysicists can gain a better understanding of the movement of the mantle and the recycling of the Earth's crust.

In conclusion, the study of noble gas distribution in the mantle is a crucial aspect of understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By studying the sources and distribution of noble gases, we can gain valuable insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. 


# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints




### Subsection: 5.1c Implications of Noble Gas Distribution

The distribution of noble gases in the mantle has significant implications for our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. The presence of noble gases in the mantle provides evidence for the early stages of the Earth's formation, when the core was still molten and releasing noble gases.

The distribution of noble gases also provides insights into the processes occurring in the mantle. For example, the higher concentrations of helium and neon in the lower mantle suggest that these gases are being transported from the core to the upper mantle through convection currents. This provides evidence for the ongoing movement and mixing of the mantle, which is crucial for the Earth's geodynamo and plate tectonics.

Furthermore, the distribution of noble gases can also be used to trace the origin of mantle plumes. Mantle plumes are columns of hot material that rise from the lower mantle to the surface. The presence of noble gases in these plumes can provide insights into their origin and evolution. For example, the presence of helium and neon in mantle plumes can suggest a core origin, while the presence of argon and krypton can suggest a crustal origin.

In conclusion, the distribution of noble gases in the mantle is a crucial aspect of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. It provides valuable insights into the early stages of the Earth's formation, the ongoing processes in the mantle, and the origin of mantle plumes. Further research and analysis of noble gases in the mantle will continue to deepen our understanding of the Earth's evolution.

### Section: 5.3 Mantle Plumes and their Noble Gas Signatures:

Mantle plumes are a crucial aspect of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. These plumes, also known as hotspots, are regions of the Earth's mantle that rise from the core-mantle boundary to the surface. They are characterized by their high temperatures and chemical heterogeneity, making them a valuable source of information for understanding the Earth's interior.

#### 5.3a Understanding Mantle Plumes

Mantle plumes are believed to originate from the core-mantle boundary, where the Earth's liquid outer core meets the solid lower mantle. As the plume rises, it carries with it a portion of the core-mantle boundary, which is enriched in iron and other elements. This enrichment is reflected in the chemical composition of the plume, making it distinct from the surrounding mantle.

The chemical and isotopic composition of basalts found at hotspots differs from mid-ocean ridge basalts. These basalts, also called ocean island basalts (OIBs), are analyzed in their radiogenic and stable isotope compositions. In radiogenic isotope systems, the originally subducted material creates diverging trends, termed mantle components. Identified mantle components are DMM (depleted mid-ocean ridge basalt (MORB) mantle), HIMU (high U/Pb-ratio mantle), EM1 (enriched mantle 1), EM2 (enriched mantle 2) and FOZO (focus zone). This geochemical signature arises from the mixing of near-surface materials such as subducted slabs and continental sediments, in the mantle source.

The processing of oceanic crust and sediments by mantle plumes can be traced using noble gases. The noble gas signature of a mantle plume is unique and can be used to identify its source. For example, the noble gas signature of a mantle plume originating from the core-mantle boundary will be distinct from a plume originating from the lower mantle. This is because the core-mantle boundary is enriched in iron and other elements, which can be detected in the noble gas signature of the plume.

The noble gas signature of a mantle plume can also provide insights into the processes occurring within the plume. For instance, the presence of certain noble gases can indicate the temperature and pressure conditions experienced by the plume during its ascent. This information can be used to better understand the dynamics of mantle plumes and their role in the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution.

In conclusion, mantle plumes play a crucial role in the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. Their unique noble gas signatures provide valuable insights into the processes occurring within the plume and its source. Further research and analysis of mantle plumes will continue to enhance our understanding of the Earth's interior and its evolution over time.





### Section: 5.3 Mantle Plumes and their Noble Gas Signatures:

Mantle plumes are a crucial aspect of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. These plumes, also known as hotspots, are regions of the Earth's mantle that rise from the core-mantle boundary to the surface. They are characterized by their high temperatures and chemical heterogeneity, making them a valuable source of information for understanding the Earth's interior.

#### 5.3b Noble Gas Signatures of Mantle Plumes

The noble gas signature of a mantle plume is a powerful tool for understanding the origin and evolution of these plumes. Noble gases, such as helium, neon, and argon, are chemically inert and do not react with other elements. This makes them ideal tracers for studying the composition of the Earth's mantle.

The noble gas signature of a mantle plume is determined by the noble gases present in the plume. These noble gases are primarily derived from the core-mantle boundary, which is enriched in iron and other elements. As the plume rises, it carries with it a portion of the core-mantle boundary, which is reflected in the noble gas composition of the plume.

The noble gas signature of a mantle plume can be used to identify its source. For example, the noble gas signature of a mantle plume originating from the core-mantle boundary will be distinct from a plume originating from the lower mantle. This is because the core-mantle boundary is enriched in iron and other elements, which can be detected in the noble gas composition of the plume.

The noble gas signature of a mantle plume can also provide insights into the processes occurring within the plume. For instance, the presence of certain noble gases can indicate the presence of melting or the interaction of the plume with other materials in the mantle.

In conclusion, the noble gas signature of a mantle plume is a valuable tool for understanding the origin and evolution of these plumes. It provides insights into the composition and processes occurring within the plume, and can be used to identify the source of the plume. Further research and analysis of noble gas signatures of mantle plumes will continue to enhance our understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

We have seen how the presence of noble gases in different layers of the Earth can provide valuable insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. We have also discussed the various techniques used to measure noble gases, such as mass spectrometry and noble gas extraction. These techniques have allowed scientists to accurately measure the composition of noble gases and track their movement through the Earth's layers. This has led to significant advancements in our understanding of the Earth's evolution, particularly in the areas of plate tectonics and the formation of the Earth's core.

Furthermore, we have examined the role of noble gases in studying the early history of the Earth. By analyzing the noble gases present in ancient rocks and minerals, scientists have been able to reconstruct the conditions of the Earth's early atmosphere and oceans. This has provided valuable insights into the processes that led to the formation of life on our planet.

In conclusion, the study of noble gases has been instrumental in our understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It has allowed us to piece together the complex puzzle of our planet's history and continue to uncover new insights into its past.

## Chapter: Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of mantle plumes and their role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Mantle plumes are columns of hot, buoyant material that rise from the Earth's core-mantle boundary to the surface. These plumes play a crucial role in the dynamics of the Earth's interior, influencing processes such as plate tectonics, volcanism, and the distribution of heat within the planet.

We will begin by exploring the basic principles of mantle plumes, including their formation and behavior. We will then delve into the various types of mantle plumes, including hotspots, large low-shear-velocity provinces (LLSVPs), and plume heads. Each type of mantle plume has its own unique characteristics and effects on the Earth's surface.

Next, we will examine the role of mantle plumes in the Earth's thermal evolution. We will discuss how mantle plumes contribute to the transfer of heat from the core to the surface, and how this process has changed over time. We will also explore the relationship between mantle plumes and the formation of hotspots, which are areas of intense volcanic activity.

Finally, we will touch upon the chemical aspects of mantle plumes. We will discuss how mantle plumes can bring up deep mantle material to the surface, providing valuable insights into the composition and evolution of the Earth's interior. We will also explore the role of mantle plumes in the formation of different types of volcanic eruptions, and how these eruptions can provide clues about the chemical makeup of mantle plumes.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of mantle plumes and their crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of mantle plumes!

## Chapter: Chapter 6: The Moon and its Evolution

### Introduction

The Moon, our closest celestial neighbor, has been a subject of fascination and study for humans since time immemorial. Its mysterious and enigmatic nature has been a source of inspiration for countless myths, legends, and scientific investigations. In this chapter, we delve into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Moon, exploring its origins, its composition, and the processes that have shaped it over billions of years.

The Moon is a unique object in our Solar System, with a history that is deeply intertwined with that of the Earth. Its formation is believed to have been a result of a giant impact between the Earth and a Mars-sized object, known as Theia. This cataclysmic event not only led to the formation of the Moon but also played a crucial role in the differentiation of the Earth's layers.

The Moon's surface, with its vast expanses of dark basaltic plains and bright highlands, offers a unique glimpse into the planet's past. The study of these surfaces, through remote sensing and direct exploration, has provided valuable insights into the Moon's thermal and chemical evolution. We will explore these insights in detail, examining the geological processes that have shaped the Moon's surface over time.

Furthermore, we will delve into the Moon's interior, exploring its structure and composition. The Moon, despite its small size, has a complex interior structure, with a solid inner core and a liquid outer core. The study of the Moon's interior has been a subject of intense research, with numerous missions and studies aimed at understanding its composition and evolution.

In this chapter, we will also explore the Moon's evolution over time, examining how it has changed from its early stages of formation to the present day. We will discuss the various theories and models proposed to explain the Moon's evolution, including the role of volcanism, impacts, and other geological processes.

Join us on this journey as we explore the Moon and its evolution, unraveling the mysteries of our closest celestial neighbor and gaining a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped our own planet.




### Subsection: 5.4a Basics of Noble Gas Diffusion

Noble gas diffusion is a fundamental process that plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It is a type of atomic diffusion that occurs in solids, where noble gases, such as helium, neon, and argon, move through the crystal lattice of a material. This process is driven by the random, thermally-activated movement of atoms, and it is governed by the principles of diffusion.

#### 5.4a.1 Diffusion Process

Diffusion is a process that results in the net transport of atoms from an area of high concentration to an area of low concentration. In the case of noble gas diffusion, the concentration gradient is created by the presence of noble gases in the Earth's mantle and crust. The rate of diffusion is determined by the diffusivity of the noble gas and the concentration gradient.

The diffusivity of a noble gas is a measure of its ability to move through a material. It is influenced by several factors, including the size and shape of the noble gas atom, the properties of the material through which it is diffusing, and the temperature. As the temperature increases, the diffusivity of the noble gas also increases, leading to an increase in the rate of diffusion.

#### 5.4a.2 In Crystals

In the crystal solid state, diffusion within the crystal lattice occurs by either interstitial or substitutional mechanisms. In interstitial lattice diffusion, a diffusant (such as C in an iron alloy), will diffuse in between the lattice structure of another crystalline element. In substitutional lattice diffusion (self-diffusion for example), the atom can only move by substituting place with another atom.

Substitutional lattice diffusion is often contingent upon the availability of point vacancies throughout the crystal lattice. Diffusing particles migrate from point vacancy to point vacancy by the rapid, essentially random jumping about. Since the prevalence of point vacancies increases in accordance with the Arrhenius equation, the rate of crystal solid state diffusion increases with temperature.

#### 5.4a.3 Random Walk Model

The movement of a single atom in a defect-free crystal can be described by the "random walk" model. In 3-dimensions, it can be shown that after $n$ jumps of length $\alpha$ the atom will have moved, on average, a distance of:

$$
\sqrt{n\alpha^2}
$$

If the jump frequency is given by $T$ (in jumps per second), the average distance moved per unit time is given by:

$$
\sqrt{T\alpha^2}
$$

This model provides a useful framework for understanding the process of noble gas diffusion in the Earth's mantle and crust. By studying the diffusion of noble gases, we can gain valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet.

### Subsection: 5.4b Implications of Noble Gas Diffusion

Noble gas diffusion has significant implications for the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. The diffusion of noble gases can provide insights into the processes occurring within the Earth's mantle and crust, and can help us understand the formation of the Earth and its evolution over time.

#### 5.4b.1 Mantle Dynamics

The diffusion of noble gases in the Earth's mantle can provide insights into the dynamics of the mantle. The mantle is a region of the Earth's interior that lies between the crust and the core. It is a solid rocky layer, but it can flow over long periods of time, which is known as mantle convection. The diffusion of noble gases can help us understand the processes that drive mantle convection, and can provide insights into the movement of heat within the Earth's interior.

The diffusion of noble gases can also provide insights into the composition of the mantle. The mantle is composed of a variety of minerals, and the diffusion of noble gases can help us understand the distribution of these minerals within the mantle. This can provide insights into the formation of the Earth's crust, and can help us understand the processes that drive the evolution of the Earth's surface.

#### 5.4b.2 Core-Mantle Interactions

The diffusion of noble gases can also provide insights into the interactions between the Earth's core and mantle. The core is a region of the Earth's interior that lies at the center of the Earth. It is composed of a liquid outer core and a solid inner core. The interaction between the core and the mantle is a key process in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

The diffusion of noble gases can help us understand the processes that drive the interaction between the core and the mantle. It can provide insights into the movement of heat from the core to the mantle, and can help us understand the processes that drive the evolution of the Earth's magnetic field.

#### 5.4b.3 Earth's Evolution

The diffusion of noble gases can also provide insights into the evolution of the Earth. The Earth is a dynamic planet, and its surface and interior have changed significantly over time. The diffusion of noble gases can help us understand the processes that have driven these changes, and can provide insights into the formation of the Earth's atmosphere and oceans.

In conclusion, the diffusion of noble gases plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It provides insights into the processes occurring within the Earth's mantle and crust, and can help us understand the formation of the Earth and its evolution over time.

### Subsection: 5.4c Case Studies of Noble Gas Diffusion

In this section, we will explore some case studies that illustrate the role of noble gas diffusion in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. These case studies will provide a more detailed understanding of the processes discussed in the previous sections.

#### 5.4c.1 The Selli Event

The Selli Event, named after the Selli Crater on Mars, is a well-studied example of a large impact event that occurred approximately 4.5 billion years ago. The impact is thought to have been caused by a Mars-sized object that collided with the Earth, creating a crater approximately 1000 km in diameter. The impact is believed to have caused significant changes in the Earth's early atmosphere, leading to a spike in the concentration of noble gases such as helium and neon.

The diffusion of these noble gases can provide insights into the processes that occurred during the Selli Event. For example, the diffusion of helium can help us understand the movement of heat from the core to the mantle, while the diffusion of neon can provide insights into the composition of the Earth's early atmosphere.

#### 5.4c.2 The Late Veneer

The Late Veneer is a hypothesized event that occurred approximately 4.5 billion years ago, where the Earth received a significant amount of material from other sources, such as comets or asteroids. This event is thought to have been responsible for the delivery of water and other volatiles to the Earth, which are essential for the development of life.

The diffusion of noble gases during the Late Veneer can provide insights into the composition of the material that was delivered to the Earth. For example, the diffusion of argon can help us understand the age of the delivered material, while the diffusion of krypton can provide insights into the processes that drove the delivery of this material to the Earth.

#### 5.4c.3 The Great Oxidation Event

The Great Oxidation Event, also known as the Oxygen Catastrophe, is a significant event in the history of the Earth. It occurred approximately 2.4 billion years ago and is thought to have been caused by the release of oxygen from photosynthetic microbes. This event led to a significant increase in the concentration of oxygen in the Earth's atmosphere, which had a profound impact on the Earth's early biosphere.

The diffusion of noble gases during the Great Oxidation Event can provide insights into the processes that drove the increase in oxygen concentration. For example, the diffusion of xenon can help us understand the movement of heat from the core to the mantle, while the diffusion of radon can provide insights into the composition of the Earth's early atmosphere.

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the importance of noble gas diffusion in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By studying the diffusion of noble gases, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of noble gas constraints and their role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. We have explored the unique properties of noble gases, their behavior in different environments, and their significance in understanding the Earth's history. 

We have learned that noble gases, being chemically inert, provide a unique window into the Earth's past. Their presence or absence, and their distribution, can reveal important information about the conditions that existed during the Earth's formation and evolution. 

We have also seen how noble gas constraints can be used to study the Earth's interior, from the core to the mantle and the crust. By analyzing the noble gases present in different layers of the Earth, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

In conclusion, noble gas constraints are a powerful tool in the study of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. They provide a unique perspective on the Earth's history, complementing other methods of study and adding depth to our understanding of our planet's past, present, and future.

The thermal evolution of the Moon is a complex and intriguing topic. The Moon's surface is marked by a myriad of craters, many of which are the result of impacts from asteroids and comets. ### Introduction

Seismic tomography is a powerful tool used to study the Earth's interior structure. It is a non-invasive method that uses seismic waves to create images of the Earth's interior. This technique has revolutionized our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution, providing insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of seismic tomography, exploring its principles, techniques, and applications. We will begin by discussing the basics of seismic waves and how they interact with the Earth's interior. We will then move on to the concept of seismic tomography, explaining how it works and its advantages over traditional methods of studying the Earth's interior.

We will also explore the various types of seismic tomography, including P-wave tomography, S-wave tomography, and ambient noise tomography. Each of these techniques offers unique insights into the Earth's interior, and we will discuss their strengths and limitations.

Furthermore, we will examine the role of seismic tomography in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. This includes studying the movement of tectonic plates, the distribution of heat in the Earth's mantle, and the composition of the Earth's core.

Finally, we will discuss the future of seismic tomography and its potential for further advancements in our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. This includes the use of new technologies and techniques, as well as the potential for seismic tomography to be used in other planetary bodies.

Join us on this journey as we explore the fascinating world of seismic tomography and its role in unraveling the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet.




### Introduction to Seismic Tomography

Seismic tomography is a powerful tool used to study the Earth's interior structure. It is a non-invasive method that uses seismic waves to create images of the Earth's interior. This technique has revolutionized our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution, providing insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of seismic tomography, exploring its principles, techniques, and applications. We will begin by discussing the basics of seismic waves and how they interact with the Earth's interior. We will then move on to the concept of seismic tomography, explaining how it works and its advantages over traditional methods of studying the Earth's interior.

We will also explore the various types of seismic tomography, including P-wave tomography, S-wave tomography, and ambient noise tomography. Each of these techniques offers unique insights into the Earth's interior, and we will discuss their strengths and limitations.

Furthermore, we will examine the role of seismic tomography in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. This includes studying the movement of tectonic plates, the distribution of heat in the Earth's mantle, and the composition of the Earth's core.

Finally, we will discuss the future of seismic tomography and its potential for further advancements in our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. This includes the use of new technologies and techniques, as well as the potential for seismic tomography to be used in other planetary bodies.

Join us on this journey as we explore the fascinating world of seismic tomography and its role in unraveling the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet.




### Section: 6.1 Constraints on Mantle Structure and Composition:

Seismic tomography has been instrumental in providing insights into the structure and composition of the Earth's mantle. The mantle, a layer between the crust and the core, is the largest layer of the Earth and plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. 

#### 6.1a Seismic Tomography and Mantle Structure

Seismic tomography has been used to image the mantle and provide insights into its structure. The mantle is not a homogeneous layer, and seismic tomography has revealed variations in its properties. These variations can be attributed to thermal and chemical differences, which are a result of processes such as mantle plumes, subducting slabs, and mineral phase changes.

One of the most significant features that can be imaged with tomography is the high velocities beneath continental shields and low velocities under ocean spreading centers. These variations in velocity can provide insights into the composition of the mantle. For instance, the high velocities beneath continental shields are often attributed to the presence of dense minerals, such as olivine and pyroxene, which are common in the upper mantle.

On the other hand, the low velocities under ocean spreading centers are often attributed to the presence of partial melt, or asthenosphere, which is less dense than the surrounding mantle. This asthenosphere is thought to be a major component of the mantle and plays a crucial role in plate tectonics.

Seismic tomography has also been used to image the mantle plume hypothesis, which proposes that areas of volcanism not readily explained by plate tectonics, called hotspots, are a result of thermal upwelling from as deep as the core-mantle boundary that become diapirs in the crust. This hypothesis is an actively contested theory, although tomographic images suggest there are anomalies beneath some hotspots.

For instance, the Yellowstone hotspot, responsible for volcanism at the Yellowstone Caldera and a series of extinct calderas along the Snake River Plain, has been imaged using seismic tomography. The Yellowstone Geodynamic Project found a strong low-velocity body from ~30 to 250 km depth beneath Yellowstone, and a weaker anomaly from 250 to 650 km depth which dipped 60° west-northwest. These features are believed to reflect the mantle plume beneath the hotspot being deflected eastward by flow in the upper mantle.

Similarly, the Hawaii hotspot, which produced the Hawaiian–Emperor seamount chain, has been imaged using seismic tomography. The tomographic images show the hotspot to be 500 to 600 km wide and up to 2,000 km deep.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the role of seismic tomography in understanding the composition of the mantle.

#### 6.1b Seismic Tomography and Mantle Composition

Seismic tomography has been instrumental in providing insights into the composition of the Earth's mantle. The mantle, being the largest layer of the Earth, plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. The variations in the properties of the mantle, such as velocity and density, can be attributed to thermal and chemical differences, which are a result of processes such as mantle plumes, subducting slabs, and mineral phase changes.

One of the most significant features that can be imaged with tomography is the high velocities beneath continental shields and low velocities under ocean spreading centers. These variations in velocity can provide insights into the composition of the mantle. For instance, the high velocities beneath continental shields are often attributed to the presence of dense minerals, such as olivine and pyroxene, which are common in the upper mantle.

On the other hand, the low velocities under ocean spreading centers are often attributed to the presence of partial melt, or asthenosphere, which is less dense than the surrounding mantle. This asthenosphere is thought to be a major component of the mantle and plays a crucial role in plate tectonics.

Seismic tomography has also been used to image the mantle plume hypothesis, which proposes that areas of volcanism not readily explained by plate tectonics, called hotspots, are a result of thermal upwelling from as deep as the core-mantle boundary that become diapirs in the crust. This hypothesis is an actively contested theory, although tomographic images suggest there are anomalies beneath some hotspots.

The Yellowstone hotspot, for instance, has been imaged using seismic tomography. The Yellowstone Geodynamic Project found a strong low-velocity body from ~30 to 250 km depth beneath Yellowstone, and a weaker anomaly from 250 to 650 km depth which dipped 60° west-northwest. These features are believed to reflect the mantle plume beneath the hotspot being deflected eastward by flow in the upper mantle seen in S-wave models.

The Hawaii hotspot, on the other hand, produced the Hawaiian–Emperor seamount chain. Tomographic images show it to be 500 to 600 km wide and up to 2,000 km deep. This suggests that the Hawaii hotspot is a large-scale feature that extends deep into the mantle.

In conclusion, seismic tomography has been a powerful tool in studying the structure and composition of the Earth's mantle. It has provided insights into the processes that shape our planet and continues to be a crucial tool in the study of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.

#### 6.1c Case Studies of Mantle Structure and Composition

In this section, we will delve deeper into the structure and composition of the Earth's mantle by examining some case studies. These case studies will provide a more detailed understanding of the mantle's properties and how they vary across different regions of the Earth.

##### The Pacific Ring of Fire

The Pacific Ring of Fire, a region of intense volcanic activity that encircles the Pacific Ocean, provides a unique opportunity to study the mantle. The region is characterized by a high frequency of earthquakes and volcanic eruptions, which are often associated with mantle plumes.

The Juan de Fuca Plate, one of the smaller plates that make up the Pacific Ring of Fire, is a prime example of a mantle plume. The plate, which is being subducted beneath the North American Plate, has been imaged using seismic tomography. The images reveal a low-velocity anomaly beneath the plate, which is believed to be a mantle plume. This plume is thought to be responsible for the volcanic activity observed in the region, including the Cascade Volcanic Arc.

##### The Mid-Atlantic Ridge

The Mid-Atlantic Ridge, a mountain range that runs along the floor of the Atlantic Ocean, is another region where seismic tomography has been used to study the mantle. The ridge is a site of mid-ocean ridge spreading, a process where new oceanic crust is formed.

Seismic tomography has revealed variations in the velocity of the mantle beneath the Mid-Atlantic Ridge. These variations are believed to be a result of the mantle being thinned by the spreading process. The images also reveal the presence of a low-velocity anomaly beneath the ridge, which is thought to be a mantle plume.

##### The Alps

The Alps, a mountain range in Europe, provide another interesting case study of the mantle. The region is characterized by high velocities beneath the continental shield, which are believed to be a result of the presence of dense minerals, such as olivine and pyroxene.

Seismic tomography has also been used to image the mantle beneath the Alps. The images reveal a high-velocity anomaly beneath the region, which is thought to be a result of the mantle being thickened by the collision of the African and European Plates.

In conclusion, these case studies provide a more detailed understanding of the mantle's properties and how they vary across different regions of the Earth. They also highlight the importance of seismic tomography in studying the mantle and understanding the processes that shape our planet.




### Subsection: 6.1c Case Studies of Seismic Tomography

In this section, we will delve into some specific case studies of seismic tomography to further illustrate the insights it provides into the structure and composition of the Earth's mantle.

#### 6.1c.1 The Hawaiian Hotspot

The Hawaiian hotspot is one of the most well-studied hotspots in the world. Seismic tomography has been instrumental in providing insights into the mantle plume beneath the Hawaiian Islands. The tomographic images suggest a large, low-shear-velocity anomaly beneath the Hawaiian Islands, which is interpreted as a mantle plume. This plume is thought to originate from the core-mantle boundary and rise to the surface, creating the Hawaiian Islands.

The tomographic images also reveal a complex structure beneath the Hawaiian Islands. The plume is surrounded by a ring of high-shear-velocity anomalies, which are interpreted as a ring of dense material that is sinking back into the mantle. This ring of sinking material is thought to be a result of the plume's interaction with the surrounding mantle.

#### 6.1c.2 The Yellowstone Hotspot

The Yellowstone hotspot is another well-studied hotspot. Seismic tomography has been used to image the mantle beneath the Yellowstone Caldera, which is the site of frequent volcanic activity. The tomographic images suggest a large, low-shear-velocity anomaly beneath the caldera, which is interpreted as a mantle plume. However, unlike the Hawaiian hotspot, the Yellowstone plume is not thought to originate from the core-mantle boundary.

The tomographic images also reveal a complex structure beneath the Yellowstone Caldera. The plume is surrounded by a ring of high-shear-velocity anomalies, which are interpreted as a ring of dense material that is sinking back into the mantle. This ring of sinking material is thought to be a result of the plume's interaction with the surrounding mantle.

#### 6.1c.3 The Mid-Atlantic Ridge

The Mid-Atlantic Ridge is a mid-ocean ridge where the Earth's crust is spreading apart. Seismic tomography has been used to image the mantle beneath the ridge. The tomographic images suggest a complex structure beneath the ridge, with both high- and low-shear-velocity anomalies. These anomalies are thought to be a result of the interaction between the spreading crust and the surrounding mantle.

In conclusion, seismic tomography has been instrumental in providing insights into the structure and composition of the Earth's mantle. The case studies discussed in this section illustrate the power of this technique in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of seismic tomography, a powerful tool for exploring the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. We have seen how this technique, which uses seismic waves to image the Earth's interior, has revolutionized our understanding of the planet's structure and dynamics. 

We have learned that seismic tomography provides a three-dimensional picture of the Earth's interior, revealing the complex interplay of thermal and chemical processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. This technique has allowed us to map the distribution of heat and chemical heterogeneities in the Earth's mantle and core, providing valuable insights into the processes of plate tectonics, mantle convection, and the generation of the Earth's magnetic field.

Moreover, we have discussed the limitations and challenges of seismic tomography, such as the need for more data and the difficulty of interpreting the results. Despite these challenges, the potential of seismic tomography for further advancing our understanding of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution is immense. As technology continues to improve and more data becomes available, we can expect seismic tomography to play an increasingly important role in our exploration of the Earth's interior.
